<td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;">
<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini">
<ul>
<li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Politics_sidebar" title="Template:Politics sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Politics_sidebar" title="Template talk:Politics sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Politics_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li>
</ul>

<p><b>Anti-Russian sentiment</b> or <b>Russophobia</b> is a diverse spectrum of negative feelings, dislikes, fears, aversion, derision and/or prejudice of <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Russians" title="Russians">Russians</a> and/or <a href="/wiki/Russian_culture" title="Russian culture">Russian culture</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p>A wide variety of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mass_culture" title="Mass culture">mass culture</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clich%C3%A9s" title="Clichés">clichés</a> about Russia and Russians exists. Many of these <a href="/wiki/Stereotype" title="Stereotype">stereotypes</a> were developed during the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-prevalence_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-prevalence-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> and were used as elements of political war against the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>. Some of these prejudices are still observed in the discussions of the relations with Russia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> Negative representation of Russia and Russians in modern popular culture is also often described as functional, as stereotypes about Russia may be used for <a href="/wiki/Framing_(social_sciences)" title="Framing (social sciences)">framing</a> reality, like creating an image of an enemy, or an excuse, or an explanation for <a href="/wiki/Compensation_(psychology)" title="Compensation (psychology)">compensatory</a> reasons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> Decades after the end of the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a>, Russians are still portrayed as "Hollywood's go-to villains".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p>On the other hand, Russian nationalists and apologists of the politics of Russia often use the allegations of "Russophobia" as a form of <a href="/wiki/Propaganda" title="Propaganda">propaganda</a> to counter the criticism of Russia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jdpz_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-jdpz-11">[11]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anti-Russian_sentiment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:March_of_Russian_barbarity_and_cholera_epidemic_to_Europe_(French_allegory).PNG"><img alt=" Barbaric Russian invasion." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1110" data-file-width="1998" height="139" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/March_of_Russian_barbarity_and_cholera_epidemic_to_Europe_%28French_allegory%29.PNG/250px-March_of_Russian_barbarity_and_cholera_epidemic_to_Europe_%28French_allegory%29.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/March_of_Russian_barbarity_and_cholera_epidemic_to_Europe_%28French_allegory%29.PNG/375px-March_of_Russian_barbarity_and_cholera_epidemic_to_Europe_%28French_allegory%29.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/March_of_Russian_barbarity_and_cholera_epidemic_to_Europe_%28French_allegory%29.PNG/500px-March_of_Russian_barbarity_and_cholera_epidemic_to_Europe_%28French_allegory%29.PNG 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:March_of_Russian_barbarity_and_cholera_epidemic_to_Europe_(French_allegory).PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Propaganda" title="Propaganda">Propaganda</a> depiction of a barbaric Russian <a href="/wiki/War_of_the_Sixth_Coalition" title="War of the Sixth Coalition">invasion</a> of Europe. Early 19th century, France.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On 19 October 1797 the <a href="/wiki/French_Directory" title="French Directory">French Directory</a> received a document from a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polish_people" title="Polish people">Polish</a> general, <a href="/wiki/Micha%C5%82_Sokolnicki" title="Michał Sokolnicki">Michał Sokolnicki</a>, entitled "Aperçu sur la Russie". This became known as the so-called "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Testament_of_Peter_the_Great" title="Testament of Peter the Great">Testament of Peter the Great</a>" and was first published in October 1812, during the Napoleonic wars, in Charles Louis-Lesur's much-read <i>Des progrès de la puissance russe</i>: this was at the behest of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Napoleon_I" title="Napoleon I">Napoleon I</a>, who ordered a series of articles to be published showing that "Europe is inevitably in the process of becoming booty for Russia".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mcnally_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-mcnally-13">[13]</a></sup> Subsequent to the Napoleonic wars, propaganda against Russia was continued by Napoleon's former confessor, Dominique Georges-Frédéric de Pradt, who in a series of books portrayed Russia as a "despotic" and "Asiatic" power hungry to conquer Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeumann2002133_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeumann2002133-14">[14]</a></sup> With reference to Russia's new constitutional laws in 1811 the <a href="/wiki/Savoy" title="Savoy">Savoyard</a> philosopher <a href="/wiki/Joseph_de_Maistre" title="Joseph de Maistre">Joseph de Maistre</a> wrote the now famous statement: "Every nation gets the government it deserves" ("Toute nation a le gouvernement qu'elle mérite").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Latham1906_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Latham1906-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1867, <a href="/wiki/Fyodor_Tyutchev" title="Fyodor Tyutchev">Fyodor Tyutchev</a>, a Russian poet, diplomat and member of <a href="/wiki/His_Imperial_Majesty%27s_Own_Chancellery" title="His Imperial Majesty's Own Chancellery">His Imperial Majesty's Own Chancellery</a>, introduced the actual term of "russophobia" in a letter to his daughter Anna Aksakova on 20 September 1867, where he applied it to a number of pro-Western <a href="/wiki/Liberalism_in_Russia" title="Liberalism in Russia">Russian liberals</a> who, pretending that they were merely following their <a href="/wiki/Liberalism" title="Liberalism">liberal</a> principles, developed a negative attitude towards their own country and always stood on a pro-Western and anti-Russian position, regardless of any changes in the Russian society and having a blind eye on any violations of these principles in the West, "violations in the sphere of justice, morality, and even civilization". He put the emphasis on the <a href="/wiki/Irrationality" title="Irrationality">irrationality</a> of this sentiment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shirinyants_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shirinyants-17">[17]</a></sup> Tyuchev saw Western anti-Russian sentiment as the result of misunderstanding caused by <a href="/wiki/East%E2%80%93West_dichotomy" title="East–West dichotomy">civilizational differences between East and West</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> Being an adherent of <a href="/wiki/Pan-Slavism" title="Pan-Slavism">Pan-Slavism</a>, he believed that the historical mission of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slavic_peoples" title="Slavic peoples">Slavic peoples</a> was to be united in a Pan-Slavic and <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Orthodox Christian</a> <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">Russian Empire</a> to preserve their Slavic identity and avoid cultural assimilation; in his lyrics <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a>, a Slavic yet <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic</a> country, was <a href="/wiki/Poetry" title="Poetry">poetically</a> referred to as <a href="/wiki/Judas_Iscariot" title="Judas Iscariot">Judas</a> among the Slavs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> The term returned into political dictionaries of the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> only in the middle 1930s. Further works by Russian academics, such as Igor Shafarevich's <i>Russophobia</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> or the treaty from the 1980s attributed the spread of russophobia to <a href="/wiki/Zionism" title="Zionism">Zionists</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jdpz_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-jdpz-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1843 the <a href="/wiki/Marquis_de_Custine" title="Marquis de Custine">Marquis de Custine</a> published his hugely successful 1800-page, four volume travelogue <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/La_Russie_en_1839" title="La Russie en 1839">La Russie en 1839</a></i>. Custine's scathing narrative reran what were by now clichés which presented Russia as a place where "the veneer of European civilization was too thin to be credible". Such was its huge success that several official and pirated editions quickly followed, as well as condensed versions and translations in German, Dutch and English. By 1846 approximately 200 thousand copies had been sold.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p>The influential British economist <a href="/wiki/John_Maynard_Keynes" title="John Maynard Keynes">John Maynard Keynes</a> wrote controversially on Russia, that the oppression in the country, rooted in the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Revolution" title="Russian Revolution">Red Revolution</a>, perhaps was "the fruit of some beastliness in the Russian nature", also attributing "cruelty and stupidity" to tyranny in both the "Old Russia" (tsarist) and "New Russia" (Soviet).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the 1930s and 1940s, <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">Adolf Hitler</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nazi_party" title="Nazi party">Nazi party</a> viewed the Soviet Union as populated by Slavs ruled by "<a href="/wiki/Jewish_Bolshevism" title="Jewish Bolshevism">Jewish Bolshevik</a>" masters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>Hitler stated in <i>Mein Kampf</i> his belief that the Russian state was the work of German elements in the state and not of the Slavs:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Here Fate itself seems desirous of giving us a sign. By handing Russia to Bolshevism, it robbed the Russian nation of that intelligentsia which previously brought about and guaranteed its existence as a state. For the organization of a Russian state formation was not the result of the political abilities of the Slavs in Russia, but only a wonderful example of the state-forming efficacity of the German element in an inferior race.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>A secret Nazi plan, the <a href="/wiki/Generalplan_Ost" title="Generalplan Ost">Generalplan Ost</a> called for the enslavement, expulsion or extermination of most Slavic peoples in Europe.</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>"Need, hunger, lack of comfort have been the Russians' lot for centuries. No false compassion, as their stomachs are perfectly extendible. Don't try to impose the German standards and to change their style of life. Their only wish is to be ruled by the Germans. [...] Help yourselves, and may God help you!"</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— "12 precepts for the German officer in the East", 1941<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-khatyn_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-khatyn-25">[25]</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>Modern anti-Russian sentiment peaked during the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a>, driven by Western fears of the Soviet role in communism's mission to take over the "<a href="/wiki/Free_World" title="Free World">Free World</a>".</p>
<p>Post-Soviet distrust of Russia and Russians is attributable to <a href="/wiki/Backlash_(sociology)" title="Backlash (sociology)">backlash</a> against the historical memory of <a href="/wiki/Russification" title="Russification">Russification</a> pursued by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Imperial_Russia" title="Imperial Russia">Imperial Russia</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>, and backlash against modern policies of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Russian_government" title="Russian government">Russian government</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-russiaprofile_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-russiaprofile-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p>Vlad Sobell<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> believed in 2007 that "Russophobic sentiment" in the West reflected the West's failure to adapt and change its historical attitude towards Russia, even as Russia had (in his view) abandoned past ideology for pragmatism, successfully driving its economic revival. With the West victorious over totalitarianism, Russia served to perpetuate the role of a needed adversary owing to its "unashamed continuity with the communist Soviet Union."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sobell_western_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-sobell_western-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Statistics">Statistics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anti-Russian_sentiment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Statistics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In October 2004, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gallup_Poll" title="Gallup Poll">International Gallup Organization</a> announced that according to its poll, anti-Russia sentiment remained fairly strong throughout Europe and the West in general. It found that Russia was the least popular G-8 country globally. The percentage of population with a "very negative" or "fairly negative" perception of Russia was 73% in <a href="/wiki/Kosovo" title="Kosovo">Kosovo</a>, 62% in <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a>, 57% in <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a>, 42% in the <a href="/wiki/Czech_Republic" title="Czech Republic">Czech Republic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Switzerland</a>, 37% in <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>, 32% in <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a> and <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a>, and 23% in <a href="/wiki/Estonia" title="Estonia">Estonia</a>. Overall, the percentage of respondents with a positive view of Russia was only 31%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HelsSan_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-HelsSan-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gmfus.org_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-gmfus.org-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to a 2014 survey by <a href="/wiki/Pew_Research_Center" title="Pew Research Center">Pew Research Center</a>, attitudes towards Russia in most countries worsened considerably during <a href="/wiki/2014_pro-Russian_unrest_in_Ukraine" title="2014 pro-Russian unrest in Ukraine">Russia's involvement in the 2014 crisis in Ukraine</a>. From 2013 to 2014, the median negative attitudes in Europe rose from 54% to 75%, and from 43% to 72% in the United States. Negative attitudes also rose compared to 2013 throughout the Middle East, Latin America, Asia and Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pew-attitudes-2014_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-pew-attitudes-2014-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<p>Negative attitudes towards Russia and frequent criticism of the Russian government in western media should not be confused with negative attitudes towards Russian people and culture. In a 2012 survey, the percentage of Russian immigrants in the EU that indicated that they had experienced racially motivated <a href="/wiki/Hate_crime" title="Hate crime">hate crimes</a> was 5%, which is less than the average of 10% reported by several groups of immigrants and ethnic minorities in the EU.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hatecrime_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-hatecrime-33">[33]</a></sup> 17% of Russian immigrants in the EU said that they had been victims of crimes the last 12 months, for example theft, attacks, frightening threats or harassment, as compared to an average of 24% among several groups of immigrants and ethnic minorities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
