<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="By_country">By country</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anti-Russian_sentiment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: By country">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Former_Soviet_Union">Former Soviet Union</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anti-Russian_sentiment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Former Soviet Union">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Armenia">Armenia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anti-Russian_sentiment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Armenia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Armenia%E2%80%93Russia_relations" title="Armenia–Russia relations">Armenia–Russia relations</a></div>
<p>Anti-Russian sentiment in Armenia has historically been very low. According to a July 2007 poll, only 2% of Armenians see Russia as a threat, as opposed to 88% who view Russia as Armenia's partner.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> According to Manvel Sargsyan, the Director of the Armenian Center of National and Strategic Research, "There are no special anti-Russian sentiments in Armenia."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> Armenia's first president <a href="/wiki/Levon_Ter-Petrosyan" title="Levon Ter-Petrosyan">Levon Ter-Petrosyan</a> stated in 2013 that anti-Russian sentiment "has never existed and still does not exist" in Armenia, except "some marginal elements and some individuals with anti-Russian sentiment."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> During the <a href="/wiki/Dissolution_of_the_Soviet_Union" title="Dissolution of the Soviet Union">dissolution of the Soviet Union</a> and rise of nationalism in the Soviet republics and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_bloc" title="Eastern bloc">Eastern bloc</a> countries, Armenian nationalists were among the few that did not "interpret Russia as their most significant threat."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> Armenian Foreign Minister <a href="/wiki/Vartan_Oskanian" title="Vartan Oskanian">Vartan Oskanian</a> stated in 2001 that "Anti-Soviet sentiment did not mean anti-Russian in Armenia's case."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>On several occasions, however, anti-Russian sentiment has been expressed in Armenia, particularly in response to real or perceived <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anti-Armenian" title="Anti-Armenian">anti-Armenian</a> actions by Russia. In June 1903, <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_II_of_Russia" title="Nicholas II of Russia">Nicholas II</a> issued a decree ordering the confiscation of all <a href="/wiki/Armenian_Apostolic_Church" title="Armenian Apostolic Church">Armenian Church</a> properties (including church-run schools) and its transfer to the <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Internal_Affairs_(Russia)" title="Ministry of Internal Affairs (Russia)">Russian Interior Ministry</a>. The decision was perceived by Armenians to be an effort of <a href="/wiki/Russification" title="Russification">Russification</a> and it met widespread popular resistance by the Russian Armenian population and led by the <a href="/wiki/Armenian_Revolutionary_Federation" title="Armenian Revolutionary Federation">Dashnak</a> and <a href="/wiki/Social_Democrat_Hunchakian_Party" title="Social Democrat Hunchakian Party">Hunchak</a> parties. This included attacks on Russian authorities in attempts to prevent the confiscation. The decree being eventually canceled in 1905.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> In more recent times, in July 1988, during the <a href="/wiki/Karabakh_movement" title="Karabakh movement">Karabakh movement</a>, the killing of an Armenian man and the injury of tens of others by the Soviet army in a violent <a href="/wiki/Zvartnots_Airport_clash" title="Zvartnots Airport clash">clash at Zvartnots Airport</a> near <a href="/wiki/Yerevan" title="Yerevan">Yerevan</a> sparked anti-Russian and anti-Soviet sentiment in the Armenian public.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/2015_Gyumri_massacre" title="2015 Gyumri massacre">2015 Gyumri massacre</a> caused anti-Russian sentiment among some Armenians from <a href="/wiki/Gyumri" title="Gyumri">Gyumri</a>, while others say the nationality of the killer is irrelevant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Azerbaijan">Azerbaijan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anti-Russian_sentiment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Azerbaijan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijan%E2%80%93Russia_relations" title="Azerbaijan–Russia relations">Azerbaijan–Russia relations</a></div>
<p>In Azerbaijani society, Russians are perceived mostly as invaders that have controlled Azerbaijan for almost 200 years with a two-year halfway break.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> For current generations, Russians are seen as direct and indirect perpetrators of the two most terrible events which have occurred in Azerbaijan's modern history. One is the <a href="/wiki/Black_January" title="Black January">Black January</a> (when Soviet soldiers entered Baku to suppress the independence movement and killed over 100 people in 1990);<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BertschCraft2013_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-BertschCraft2013-46">[46]</a></sup> the other is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Khojali_massacre" title="Khojali massacre">Khojali massacre</a> during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Karabakh_War" title="Karabakh War">Karabakh War</a>.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Georgia">Georgia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anti-Russian_sentiment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Georgia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Georgia%E2%80%93Russia_relations" title="Georgia–Russia relations">Georgia–Russia relations</a></div>
<p>According to a 2012 poll, 35% of Georgians perceive Russia as Georgia's biggest enemy, while the percentage was significantly higher in 2011, at 51%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-css.ethz.ch_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-css.ethz.ch-47">[47]</a></sup> In a February 2013 poll, 63% of Georgians said Russia is Georgia's biggest political and economic threat as opposed to 35% of those who looked at Russia as the most important partner for Georgia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<p>"ღრუსი" (ghrusi) is a Georgian slur, created by combinations of words "ghori" (pig) and "rusi" (Russian) and is widely used in Georgian Internet forums</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Estonia">Estonia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anti-Russian_sentiment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Estonia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Estonia%E2%80%93Russia_relations" title="Estonia–Russia relations">Estonia–Russia relations</a>, <a href="/wiki/Russians_in_Estonia" title="Russians in Estonia">Russians in Estonia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Human_rights_in_Estonia" title="Human rights in Estonia">Human rights in Estonia</a></div>
<p>According to veteran German author, journalist and Russia-correspondent <a href="/wiki/Gabriele_Krone-Schmalz" title="Gabriele Krone-Schmalz">Gabriele Krone-Schmalz</a>, there is deep disapproval of everything Russian in Estonia,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-russland_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-russland-49">[49]</a></sup> this however has been challenged in a poll conducted by Gallup International<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HelsSan_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-HelsSan-29">[29]</a></sup> which suggests that 23% of Estonians see Russia in negative light while 34% have positive attitude towards Russia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HelsSan_29-2"><a href="#cite_note-HelsSan-29">[29]</a></sup> In a 2012 poll, 3% of the Russian minority in Estonia reported that they had experienced a racially motivated hate crime (as compared to an average of 10% among immigrants in EU).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hatecrime_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-hatecrime-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to Estonian philosopher <a href="/wiki/Jaan_Kaplinski" title="Jaan Kaplinski">Jaan Kaplinski</a>, the birth of anti-Russian sentiment in Estonia dates back to 1940, as there was little or none during the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">czarist</a> and first independence period, when <a href="/wiki/Anti-German_sentiment" title="Anti-German sentiment">anti-German sentiment</a> predominated. Kaplinski states the imposition of Soviet rule in 1940 and subsequent <a href="/wiki/Soviet_deportations_from_Estonia" title="Soviet deportations from Estonia">actions by Soviet authorities</a> led to the replacement of anti-German sentiment with anti-Russian sentiment within just one year, and characterized it as "one of the greatest achievements of the Soviet authorities".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Subrenat2004_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Subrenat2004-50">[50]</a></sup> Kaplinski supposes that anti-Russian sentiment could disappear as quickly as anti-German sentiment did in 1940, however he believes the prevailing sentiment in Estonia is sustained by Estonia's politicians who employ "the use of anti-Russian sentiments in political combat," together with the "tendentious attitude of the [Estonian] media."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Subrenat2004_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-Subrenat2004-50">[50]</a></sup> Kaplinski says that a "rigid East-West attitude is to be found to some degree in Estonia when it comes to Russia, in the form that everything good comes from the West and everything bad from the East";<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Subrenat2004_50-2"><a href="#cite_note-Subrenat2004-50">[50]</a></sup> this attitude, in Kaplinski's view, "probably does not date back further than 1940 and presumably originates from Nazi propaganda."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Subrenat2004_50-3"><a href="#cite_note-Subrenat2004-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Latvia">Latvia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anti-Russian_sentiment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Latvia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Latvia%E2%80%93Russia_relations" title="Latvia–Russia relations">Latvia–Russia relations</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_Russians_in_Latvia" title="History of Russians in Latvia">History of Russians in Latvia</a></div>
<p>According to <a href="/wiki/Andrei_Tsygankov" title="Andrei Tsygankov">Andrei Tsygankov</a> (a Russian himself), ethnic Russians in Latvia are subjected to ethnic discrimination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tsygankov_p58_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-tsygankov_p58-51">[51]</a></sup> In a 2012 poll, 2% of the Russian minority in Latvia reported that they had experienced a racially motivated <a href="/wiki/Hate_crime" title="Hate crime">hate crime</a> (as compared to an average of 10% among immigrants and minorities in EU).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hatecrime_33-2"><a href="#cite_note-hatecrime-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latvian_American" title="Latvian American">Latvian American</a> doctor and former member of the <a href="/wiki/Civic_Union_(Latvia)" title="Civic Union (Latvia)">Civic Union</a>, Aivars Slucis wrote op-eds in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_York_Times" title="New York Times">New York Times</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Washington_Post" title="Washington Post">Washington Post</a> in which he explains to the Americans that Russians have invading other nations in their genes, and they can only understand the language of force. Slucis wrote that he would personally never treat a Russian patient with soviet symbols tattooed on his body who came into his office.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tsygankov_p44_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-tsygankov_p44-52">[52]</a></sup> In November 2010, <a href="/wiki/%C4%A2irts_Valdis_Kristovskis" title="Ģirts Valdis Kristovskis">Ģirts Valdis Kristovskis</a>, the Latvian Foreign Affairs Minister, became embroiled in a scandal with Slucis after email correspondence between the two from 2009 was released by journalist Lato Lapsa. In one of the letters, Slucis stated that he would not be able to treat Russians with the same level of care that we would Latvians, and also stated that in the event of a shortage of medical supplies he would deny Russians the right to access to those supplies. In reply, Kristovskis stated that he approved of "both his assessment and vision of the situation".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> According to Lapsa, Kristovskis was also in agreeance with Slucis advocating for freezing and reviewing all citizenships granted after 1991 with the thought of rescinding a majority.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kasjauns_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-kasjauns-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Northern_Caucasus">Northern Caucasus</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anti-Russian_sentiment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Northern Caucasus">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In a report by the <a href="/wiki/Jamestown_Foundation" title="Jamestown Foundation">Jamestown Foundation</a>, dealing with the topic of the (extremely positive according to the report) reception of American <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Republican Party (United States)">Republican</a> senator <a href="/wiki/John_McCain" title="John McCain">John McCain</a>'s statements about Russia's "double standards in the Caucasus" (referring to how Russia recognized <a href="/wiki/South_Ossetia" title="South Ossetia">South Ossetia</a> but would not let Chechnya go), one Chechen was quoted to have gone so far as to tell the website that Chechnya "cannot exist within the borders of Russia because every 50 years... Russia kills us Chechens".,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> demonstrating local fear of the Russian government.</p>
<p>Journalist <a href="/wiki/Fatima_Tlisova" title="Fatima Tlisova">Fatima Tlisova</a> released an article in 2009 discussing the frequent occurrences of Russian Orthodox crosses being sawed off buildings and thrown off mountains in Circassia, due to the cross being associated with the people who initiated the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muhajir_(Caucasus)" title="Muhajir (Caucasus)">mass expulsions of Circassians</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ukraine">Ukraine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anti-Russian_sentiment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Ukraine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:AntiRussianPoster.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="282" data-file-width="200" height="282" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/c/c9/AntiRussianPoster.jpg" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:AntiRussianPoster.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Modern Anti-swearing poster in <a href="/wiki/Lviv" title="Lviv">Lviv</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a>, issued by the Ukrainian nationalist political party <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/All-Ukrainian_Union_%22Freedom%22" title='All-Ukrainian Union "Freedom"'>Svoboda</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cs_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-cs-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kyivpost_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-kyivpost-59">[59]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ukrainian_language" title="Ukrainian language">Ukrainian</a> text reads: "<i>Remember! <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Russian_mat" title="Russian mat">Swearing</a> turns you into a <a href="/wiki/Moskal" title="Moskal">Moskal</a></i> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ethnic_slur" title="Ethnic slur">ethnic slur</a>, means "Russian").</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Russia%E2%80%93Ukraine_relations" title="Russia–Ukraine relations">Russia–Ukraine relations</a></div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Russians_in_Ukraine" title="Russians in Ukraine">Russians in Ukraine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Boycott_Russian_Films" title="Boycott Russian Films">Boycott Russian Films</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Do_not_buy_Russian_goods!" title="Do not buy Russian goods!">Do not buy Russian goods!</a></div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/De-Russification#Ukraine" title="De-Russification">De-Russification § Ukraine</a></div>
<p>During the presidency of Viktor Yushchenko (2005-2010) anti-Russian statements became common in the media, in particular, aired by right-wing politicians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<p>In a poll held by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kyiv_International_Institute_of_Sociology" title="Kyiv International Institute of Sociology">Kyiv International Institute of Sociology</a> in May 2009 in <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a>, 96% of respondents were positive about <a href="/wiki/Russians" title="Russians">Russians</a> as an ethnic group, 93% respected the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Russian_Federation" title="Russian Federation">Russian Federation</a> and 76% respected the Russian establishment. In a poll held by <a href="/wiki/Levada_Center" title="Levada Center">Levada Center</a> in <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a> in June 2009, 75% of respondents respected <a href="/wiki/Ukrainians" title="Ukrainians">Ukrainians</a> as an ethnic group, but 55% were negative about Ukraine as the state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Levada_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Levada-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to the statistics released on October 21, 2010 by the Institute of Sociology of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Academy_of_Science_of_Ukraine" title="National Academy of Science of Ukraine">National Academy of Science of Ukraine</a>, positive attitudes towards Russians have been decreasing since 1994. In response to a question gauging tolerance of Russians, 15% of Western Ukrainians responded positively. In Central Ukraine, 30% responded positively (from 60% in 1994); 60% responded positively in Southern Ukraine (from 70% in 1994); and 64% responded positively in Eastern Ukraine (from 75% in 1994). Furthermore, 6-7% of Western Ukrainians would banish Russians entirely from Ukraine, and 7-8% in Central Ukraine responded similarly. This level of sentiment was not found in Southern or Eastern Ukraine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zikios_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-zikios-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<p>The right-wing political party <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/All-Ukrainian_Union_%22Svoboda%22" title='All-Ukrainian Union "Svoboda"'>"Svoboda"</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cs_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-cs-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kyivpost_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-kyivpost-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> has invoked radical Russophobic rhetoric<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> (see poster) and has electoral support enough to garner majority support in local councils,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> as seen in the <a href="/wiki/Ternopil_Oblast_local_election,_2009" title="Ternopil Oblast local election, 2009">Ternopil regional council</a> in Western Ukraine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TernopileleSvo_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-TernopileleSvo-66">[66]</a></sup> Analysts explained Svoboda’s victory in <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Galicia" title="Eastern Galicia">Eastern Galicia</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Ukrainian_local_elections,_2010" title="Ukrainian local elections, 2010">2010 Ukrainian local elections</a> as a result of the policies of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Azarov_Government" title="Azarov Government">Azarov Government</a> who were seen as too <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pro-Russian" title="Pro-Russian">pro-Russian</a> by the voters of "Svoboda".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Svovic2010_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Svovic2010-67">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Oleh_Tyahnybok" title="Oleh Tyahnybok">Oleh Tyahnybok</a>, the leader of the far-right <a href="/wiki/Svoboda_(political_party)" title="Svoboda (political party)">Svoboda</a> party, whose members hold senior positions in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cabinet_of_Ukraine" title="Cabinet of Ukraine">Ukraine's government</a>, urged his party to fight "the Moscow-Jewish mafia" ruling Ukraine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Right_Sector" title="Right Sector">Right Sector</a>'s leader for West Ukraine, <a href="/wiki/Oleksandr_Muzychko" title="Oleksandr Muzychko">Oleksandr Muzychko</a>, has talked about fighting "communists, Jews and Russians for as long as blood flows in my veins."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> Ukrainian–Israeli oligarch and governor of Dnipropetrovsk <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ihor_Kolomoisky" title="Ihor Kolomoisky">Ihor Kolomoisky</a> issued a $10,000 bounty for the apprehension of Russian 'saboteurs'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> At least one billboard exists with the following text: "$10,000 for a <a href="/wiki/Moskal" title="Moskal">Moskal</a>" (derogatory name for Russians).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Former_Eastern_Bloc">Former Eastern Bloc</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anti-Russian_sentiment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Former Eastern Bloc">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Czech_Republic">Czech Republic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anti-Russian_sentiment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Czech Republic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Czech_Republic%E2%80%93Russia_relations" title="Czech Republic–Russia relations">Czech Republic–Russia relations</a></div>
<p>The attitude to Russia and Russians sharply deteriorated in <a href="/wiki/Czechoslovakia" title="Czechoslovakia">Czechoslovakia</a> after the <a href="/wiki/Warsaw_Pact_invasion_of_Czechoslovakia" title="Warsaw Pact invasion of Czechoslovakia">Soviet-led military crackdown</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Prague_Spring" title="Prague Spring">Prague Spring</a> in 1968.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Poland">Poland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anti-Russian_sentiment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Poland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Poland%E2%80%93Russia_relations" title="Poland–Russia relations">Poland–Russia relations</a></div>
<p>According to a 2013 <a href="/wiki/BBC_World_Service" title="BBC World Service">BBC World Service</a> poll, 19% of Poles viewed Russia's influence positively, with 49% expressing a negative view.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<p>Mutual animosity between Poland and Russia have a long history, <a href="/wiki/Boles%C5%82aw_I%27s_intervention_in_the_Kievan_succession_crisis" title="Bolesław I's intervention in the Kievan succession crisis">with military clashes tracing as back as</a> to the times of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kievan_Rus" title="Kievan Rus">Kievan Rus</a>. Since the late <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Poland_(1385%E2%80%931569)" title="Kingdom of Poland (1385–1569)">Kingdom of Poland</a> allied with the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Duchy_of_Lithuania" title="Grand Duchy of Lithuania">Grand Duchy of Lithuania</a> which <a href="/wiki/Muscovite%E2%80%93Lithuanian_Wars" title="Muscovite–Lithuanian Wars">struggled with</a> the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grand_Duchy_of_Muscovy" title="Grand Duchy of Muscovy">Grand Duchy of Muscovy</a>. Over centuries, there have been several <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polish-Russian_wars" title="Polish-Russian wars">Polish-Russian wars</a>, with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polish%E2%80%93Muscovite_War_(1605%E2%80%931618)#Poles_in_Moscow_.281610.29" title="Polish–Muscovite War (1605–1618)">Poland once occupying Moscow</a> and later <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Russian_partition" title="Russian partition">Russians controlling much of Poland in the 19th century</a> as well as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic_of_Poland" title="People's Republic of Poland">in the 20th century</a>.</p>
<p>Much of the modern anti-Russian feelings in Poland is caused by grievances of the past.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RFE_Warsaw_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-RFE_Warsaw-75">[75]</a></sup> The most contentious issue is the massacre of 22,000 Polish officers, priests and intellectuals in <a href="/wiki/Katyn_massacre" title="Katyn massacre">Katyn Forest</a> in 1940, and deportation of around 250,000 mostly Polish civilians and others including soldiers to Siberia and Kazakhstan where many, around 100,000 died, even though the Russian government has officially acknowledged and apologized for the atrocity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2005 <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i> reported after the Polish daily <i><a href="/wiki/Gazeta_Wyborcza" title="Gazeta Wyborcza">Gazeta Wyborcza</a></i> that "relations between Poland and Russia are as bad as ever".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT2005_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT2005-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
<p>Jakub Boratyński, the director of international programs at the independent Polish think tank <a href="/wiki/Stefan_Batory_Foundation" title="Stefan Batory Foundation">Stefan Batory Foundation</a>, said in 2005 that anti-Russian feelings have substantially decreased since Poland joined the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">EU</a> and <a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">NATO</a>, and that Poles feel more secure than before, but he also admitted that many people in Poland still look suspiciously at Russian foreign-policy moves and are afraid Russia is seeking to <i>"recreate an empire in a different form."</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RFE_Warsaw_75-1"><a href="#cite_note-RFE_Warsaw-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Romania">Romania</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anti-Russian_sentiment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Romania">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Romania%E2%80%93Russia_relations" title="Romania–Russia relations">Romania–Russia relations</a></div>
<p>Anti-Russian sentiment dates back to the conflict between the Russian and Ottoman empires in the 18th and early 19th centuries and the ceding of part of the <a href="/wiki/Bessarabia" title="Bessarabia">Moldavian principality</a> to Russia by the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> in 1812 after its <i>de facto</i> annexation, and to the annexations during World War II and after by the Soviet Union of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Northern_Bukovina" title="Northern Bukovina">Northern Bukovina</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bessarabia" title="Bessarabia">Bessarabia</a> and the policies of ethnic cleansing, Russification and deportations that have taken place in those territories against ethnic Romanians. Following WWII, Romania, an ally of <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Nazi Germany</a>, was occupied by Soviet forces. Soviet dominance over the Romanian economy was manifested through the so-called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sovroms" title="Sovroms">Sovroms</a>, exacting a tremendous economic toll ostensibly as war-time reparations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rest_of_the_world">Rest of the world</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anti-Russian_sentiment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Rest of the world">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Australia">Australia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anti-Russian_sentiment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Australia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>According to the World Public Opinion poll undertaken in 2013, 53% of Australians had a negative view of Russia, up 15% from 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WPO_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-WPO-83">[83]</a></sup> Following the shooting down of <a href="/wiki/Malaysia_Airlines_Flight_17" title="Malaysia Airlines Flight 17">Malaysia Airlines Flight 17</a>, allegedly by Russian-backed separatists in <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Ukraine" title="Eastern Ukraine">eastern Ukraine</a>, which claimed the lives of at least 27 Australians, the Australian government and the Opposition became decidedly anti-Russian, with Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Tony_Abbott" title="Tony Abbott">Tony Abbott</a> considering barring Russian President <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Putin" title="Vladimir Putin">Vladimir Putin</a> from attending the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2014_G-20_Brisbane_summit" title="2014 G-20 Brisbane summit">2014 G-20 Brisbane summit</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Finland">Finland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anti-Russian_sentiment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Finland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Russians_in_Finland#Manifestations_of_intolerance" title="Russians in Finland">Russians in Finland § Manifestations of intolerance</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Finland%E2%80%93Russia_relations" title="Finland–Russia relations">Finland–Russia relations</a></div>
<p>In Finland, anti-Russian sentiment has been studied since the 1970s. The history of anti-Russian sentiment has two main theories. One of them claims that Finns and Russians have been archenemies throughout history. The position is considered to have been dominated at least the 1700s since the days of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greater_Wrath" title="Greater Wrath">Greater Wrath</a>, when the Russians "occupied Finland and raped it." This view largely assumes that through the centuries, "Russia is a violent slayer and Finland is an innocent, virginal victim".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> In the 1920s and 1930s this anti-Russian and anti-Communism propaganda had a fertile ground.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> Failed Russian actions to <a href="/wiki/Russification_of_Finland" title="Russification of Finland">terminate Finnish autonomy and cultural uniqueness</a> (1899–1905 and 1908–1917) contributed greatly to both the anti-Russian feelings in Finland. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Non-Aggression_between_Germany_and_the_Soviet_Union" title="Treaty of Non-Aggression between Germany and the Soviet Union">Molotov–Ribbentrop Pact</a>, which granted Finland to Soviet Union, followed the attack of the Soviet Union against Finland during the <a href="/wiki/Winter_War" title="Winter War">Winter War</a> and Soviet annexation of large parts of Finland. This caused high casualties among the Finns and 11% of the total population had to leave their homes, later causing great bitterness, and has endured as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Karelian_question_in_Finnish_politics" title="Karelian question in Finnish politics">Karelian question in Finnish politics</a>.</p>
<p>Another theory considers anti-Russian sentiment as being born in Finland at the time of <a href="/wiki/Finnish_Civil_War" title="Finnish Civil War">civil war</a> 1917–1918, and the anti-Russian political and ideological <a href="/wiki/White_Finland" title="White Finland">White Finland</a> created a confrontation which deliberately blew and spread the sentiment. Anti-Russian sentiment was created against the external threat of the Soviet Union and it was considered almost a national duty in the 1920s and 1930s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> During <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Front_(World_War_II)" title="Eastern Front (World War II)">World War II</a>, Finns organized <a href="/wiki/East_Karelian_concentration_camps" title="East Karelian concentration camps">internment camps</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Finnish_military_administration_in_Eastern_Karelia" title="Finnish military administration in Eastern Karelia">occupied East Karelia</a> where ethnic segregation between 'relatives' (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Finnic_peoples" title="Finnic peoples">Finnic population</a>) and 'non-relatives' (other, primarily Russian population) took place which has been attributed to anti-Russian sentiment.</p>
<p>According to polls in 2004, 62% of Finnish citizens had a negative view of Russia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HelsSan_29-3"><a href="#cite_note-HelsSan-29">[29]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ingrian_Finns#Ingrians_in_the_Soviet_Union" title="Ingrian Finns">Deportation</a> of <a href="/wiki/Ingrian_Finns" title="Ingrian Finns">Ingrian Finns</a>, indigenous to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St._Petersburg" title="St. Petersburg">St. Petersburg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ingria" title="Ingria">Ingria</a>, and other Soviet repressions against its Finnish minorities have contributed to negative views of Russia. In a 2012 poll, 12% of Russian immigrants in Finland reported that they had experienced a racially motivated hate crime (as compared to an average of 10% of immigrants in the EU).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hatecrime_33-3"><a href="#cite_note-hatecrime-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sweden">Sweden</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anti-Russian_sentiment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Sweden">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dra_at_helvete.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="3072" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Dra_at_helvete.jpg/220px-Dra_at_helvete.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Dra_at_helvete.jpg/330px-Dra_at_helvete.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Dra_at_helvete.jpg/440px-Dra_at_helvete.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dra_at_helvete.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Dra åt helvete</i> ("Go to hell") in a Swedish university students' book of drinking songs printed 2007, written by a Finnish student in remembrance of <a href="/wiki/Nikolay_Bobrikov" title="Nikolay Bobrikov">Nikolay Bobrikov</a>. Translation on description page.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Russia%E2%80%93Sweden_relations" title="Russia–Sweden relations">Russia–Sweden relations</a> and <a href="/wiki/Russians_in_Sweden" title="Russians in Sweden">Russians in Sweden</a></div>
<p>The Swedish words <i>russofob</i> (Russophobe) and <i>russofobi</i> (Russophobia) were first recorded in 1877 and 1904 respectively and its more frequent synonym <i>rysskräck</i> (fear of Russia or Russians) in 1907. Older synonyms were <i>rysshat</i> (hatred of Russia or Russians) from 1846 and <i>ryssantipati</i> (antipathy against Russia or Russians) from 1882.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Saob_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-Saob-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Russian state is said to have been organized in the 9th century AD at <a href="/wiki/Veliky_Novgorod" title="Veliky Novgorod">Novgorod</a> by <a href="/wiki/Rurik" title="Rurik">Rurik</a>, supposedly coming from Sweden. In the 13th century, <a href="/wiki/Stockholm" title="Stockholm">Stockholm</a> was founded to stop foreign navies from invading lake <a href="/wiki/M%C3%A4laren" title="Mälaren">Mälaren</a>. Both events are signs that hostile naval missions across the <a href="/wiki/Baltic_Sea" title="Baltic Sea">Baltic Sea</a> go a long way back, temporarily ending with the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_N%C3%B6teborg" title="Treaty of Nöteborg">peace treaty of Nöteborg</a> 1323 between Sweden and the <a href="/wiki/Novgorod_Republic" title="Novgorod Republic">Novgorod Republic</a> (which later became Russia), soon to be broken by another <a href="/wiki/Swedish%E2%80%93Novgorodian_Wars" title="Swedish–Novgorodian Wars">catholic Swedish crusade into Greek-orthodox Novgorod</a>. Russia has been described as Sweden's "<a href="/wiki/Archenemy" title="Archenemy">archenemy</a>" (a title also given to <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a>). The two countries have often been at war, most intensively during the <a href="/wiki/Great_Northern_War" title="Great Northern War">Great Northern War</a> (1700–1721) and the <a href="/wiki/Finnish_War" title="Finnish War">Finnish War</a> (1808–1809), when Sweden lost that third of its territory to Russia that now is <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a>. Sweden defeated a Russian army in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Narva_(1700)" title="Battle of Narva (1700)">Battle of Narva (1700)</a>, but was defeated by Russia in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Poltava" title="Battle of Poltava">Battle of Poltava</a> 1709. In 1719 Russian troops burnt most Swedish cities and industrial communities along the Baltic sea coast to the ground (from Norrköping up to Piteå in the north) in what came to be called "Rysshärjningarna" (the Russian ravages, a term first recorded in 1730<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Saob_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-Saob-89">[89]</a></sup>). "The Russians are coming" (<i>ryssen kommer</i>) is a traditional Swedish warning call.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> After the death of king <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Charles_XII" title="Charles XII">Charles XII</a> in 1718 and the peace in 1721, Swedish politics was dominated by a peace-minded parliament, with a more aggressive opposition (<a href="/wiki/Age_of_Liberty#Hats_and_Caps" title="Age of Liberty">Hats and Caps</a>). When Swedish officer <a href="/wiki/Malcolm_Sinclair_(Swedish_nobleman)" title="Malcolm Sinclair (Swedish nobleman)">Malcolm Sinclair</a> was murdered in 1739 by two Russian officers, the anti-Russian ballad <i>Sinclairsvisan</i> by Anders Odel became very popular.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup></p>
<p>After 1809, there have been no more wars between Russia and Sweden, partly due to <a href="/wiki/Swedish_neutrality" title="Swedish neutrality">Swedish neutrality</a> and nonalignment foreign policy since then. Peaceful relationships and the Russian capital being <a href="/wiki/Saint_Petersburg" title="Saint Petersburg">Saint Petersburg</a>, many Swedish companies ran large businesses in Imperial Russia, including <a href="/wiki/Branobel" title="Branobel">Branobel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ericsson" title="Ericsson">Ericsson</a>. Many poets still grieved the loss of Finland and called for a military revenge,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> ideas that were refueled by the <a href="/wiki/Crimean_War" title="Crimean War">Crimean War</a> in the 1850s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> With the increasing cultural exchange between neighboring countries (<a href="/wiki/Scandinavism" title="Scandinavism">Scandinavism</a>) and the nationalist revival in Finland (through <a href="/wiki/Johan_Ludvig_Runeberg" title="Johan Ludvig Runeberg">Johan Ludvig Runeberg</a> and <a href="/wiki/Elias_L%C3%B6nnrot" title="Elias Lönnrot">Elias Lönnrot</a>), contempt with the attempts of <a href="/wiki/Russification_of_Finland" title="Russification of Finland">Russification of Finland</a> spread to Sweden. Before <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, travelling Russian saw filers were suspected of espionage by Swedish proponents of increased military spending. After the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Revolution" title="Russian Revolution">Russian Revolution</a> in the spring of 1917 and the abdication of the Tsar, great hope was vested in the new <a href="/wiki/Russian_Provisional_Government" title="Russian Provisional Government">provisional government</a>, only to be replaced with despair after the so-called <a href="/wiki/October_Revolution" title="October Revolution">October Revolution</a>. Old anti-Russian sentiments were compounded by a fresh element of <a href="/wiki/Anti-communism" title="Anti-communism">anti-communism</a>, to last for the duration of the existence of the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>. Many Swedes voluntarily joined the Finnish side in the <a href="/wiki/Winter_War" title="Winter War">Winter War</a> between Finland and Soviet Union 1939–1940. When the Soviet state was finally dissolved in 1991, anti-communism became less relevant in terms of power politics and for some time, few seemed to recall the fear of its predecessor. The old cry <i>ryssen kommer</i> also seemed obsolete.</p>
<p>Thus, in statements made by Swedish politicians, the Swedish sentiments against the Russian government have always been about fear of military invasion, which now seemed to be gone for the foreseeable future, and also about <a href="/wiki/Human_rights_in_Russia" title="Human rights in Russia">human rights</a> and democracy issues. Only 31% of Swedes stated that they liked Russia in 2011, and 23% in 2012, and only 10% have confidence in Russian elections.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gmfus.org_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-gmfus.org-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p>In June 2014, political scientist <a href="/wiki/Sergey_Alexandrovich_Markov" title="Sergey Alexandrovich Markov">Sergey Markov</a> complained about Russophobia in Sweden and Finland, comparing it to <a href="/wiki/Antisemitism" title="Antisemitism">antisemitism</a>. "Would you want to be part of starting a Third World War? Antisemitism started the Second World War, Russophobia could start a third.", he commented.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> The retired Swedish history professor and often cited expert on Russia <a href="/wiki/Kristian_Gerner" title="Kristian Gerner">Kristian Gerner</a> said he was "almost shocked" by Markov's claim, and described his worldview as "nearly paranoid".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="France">France</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anti-Russian_sentiment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: France">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/France%E2%80%93Russia_relations" title="France–Russia relations">France–Russia relations</a></div>
<p>Anti-Russian sentiment was common in France after the French defeat by the Russians in the <a href="/wiki/French_invasion_of_Russia" title="French invasion of Russia">1812 War</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="New_Zealand">New Zealand</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anti-Russian_sentiment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: New Zealand">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The history of Anti-Russian sentiment in New Zealand was analyzed in Glynn Barratt's book <i>Russophobia in New Zealand, 1838-1908</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-barrat_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-barrat-99">[99]</a></sup> This research was expanded to cover the period up to 1939 in an article by Tony Wilson.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wilson_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-wilson-100">[100]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to Wilson, Russophobia towards the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">Russian Empire</a> had no roots in the country itself and was fueled by British Russophobic attitude, New Zealand being a British colony. It was aggravated by lack of information about Russia and contacts with it due to the mutual remoteness. Various wars involving the Russian Empire fueled the <a href="/wiki/Coastal_fortifications_of_New_Zealand#The_.22Russian-scare.22_forts_of_1885" title="Coastal fortifications of New Zealand">"Russian scare"</a>. Additional negative attitude was brought by Jewish immigration after <a href="/wiki/Anti-Jewish_pogroms_in_the_Russian_Empire" title="Anti-Jewish pogroms in the Russian Empire">Anti-Jewish pogroms in the Russian Empire</a>, which was halted as a combined result of Russophobia and anti-Semitism. As of 1916, there were 1242 settlers of Russian origin in the country, including 169 Jews. During <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a> Russophobia was temporarily supplanted by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Germanophobia" title="Germanophobia">Germanophobia</a> for evident reasons; however, soon after the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Russian_Revolution_of_1917" title="Russian Revolution of 1917">Russian Revolution of 1917</a>, the fear of <a href="/wiki/Marxism" title="Marxism">Marxism</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bolshevism" title="Bolshevism">Bolshevism</a> revived Russophobia in the form of "<a href="/wiki/Red_Scare" title="Red Scare">Red Scare</a>". Notably, local Russians had no issues with Russophobia. By late 1920s pragmatism moderated Russophobia in official circles, especially during the <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a>. Sympathetic views were propagated by visitors to the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/George_Bernard_Shaw" title="George Bernard Shaw">George Bernard Shaw</a>, impressed by <a href="/wiki/Propaganda_in_the_Soviet_Union" title="Propaganda in the Soviet Union">Soviet propaganda</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wilson_100-1"><a href="#cite_note-wilson-100">[100]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="United_Kingdom">United Kingdom</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anti-Russian_sentiment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: United Kingdom">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Russia%E2%80%93United_Kingdom_relations" title="Russia–United Kingdom relations">Russia–United Kingdom relations</a></div>
<p>In Great Britain, anti-Russian sentiment arose during conflicts including the Crimean War<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cain_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-cain-101">[101]</a></sup> and the Anglo-Afghan wars, which were seen as representing Russia's territorial ambitions regarding the British empire in India. This competition for <a href="/wiki/Sphere_of_influence" title="Sphere of influence">spheres of influence</a> and colonies (see e.g. <a href="/wiki/The_Great_Game" title="The Great Game">The Great Game</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Berlin_Congress" title="Berlin Congress">Berlin Congress</a>) fueled anti-Russian sentiment in <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Great Britain</a>. British <a href="/wiki/Propaganda" title="Propaganda">propaganda</a> of the time took up the theme of Russians as uncultured <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asiatic</a> barbarians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> The American professor Jimmie E. Cain Jr has claimed that these views were then exported to other parts of the world and were reflected in the literature of late the 19th and early 20th centuries,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cain_101-1"><a href="#cite_note-cain-101">[101]</a></sup> though this is to miss the fact that these views had been formulated much earlier and already widely published by various French sources since 1812.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Iran">Iran</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anti-Russian_sentiment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Iran">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Russia_relations" title="Iran–Russia relations">Iran–Russia relations</a></div>
<p>Due to Russia's support of the Iranian government, many protesters started chants of "Death to Russia" after the <a href="/wiki/Iranian_presidential_election,_2009" title="Iranian presidential election, 2009">2009 Presidential election</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Japan">Japan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anti-Russian_sentiment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Japan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Japan%E2%80%93Russia_relations" title="Japan–Russia relations">Japan–Russia relations</a> and <a href="/wiki/Russians_in_Japan" title="Russians in Japan">Russians in Japan</a></div>
<p>Most Japanese interaction with Russian individuals – besides in major cities such as <a href="/wiki/Tokyo" title="Tokyo">Tokyo</a> – happens with seamen and fishermen of the <a href="/wiki/Fishing_industry_in_Russia" title="Fishing industry in Russia">Russian fishing fleet</a>, therefore Japanese people tend to carry the <a href="/wiki/Stereotype" title="Stereotype">stereotypes</a> associated with sailors over to Russians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> According to a 2012 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pew_Global_Attitudes_Project" title="Pew Global Attitudes Project">Pew Global Attitudes Project</a> survey, 72% of Japanese people view Russia unfavorably, compared with 22% who viewed it favorably, making Japan the most anti-Russian country surveyed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PewAttitudesProject2012_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-PewAttitudesProject2012-106">[106]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Turkey">Turkey</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anti-Russian_sentiment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Turkey">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Russia%E2%80%93Turkey_relations" title="Russia–Turkey relations">Russia–Turkey relations</a></div>
<p>According to a 2013 survey 73% of Turks look at Russia unfavorably against 16% with favorable views.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the 20th century, anti-Russian sentiment in Turkey was so great that the Russians refused to allow a Turkish military attache to accompany their armies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">United States</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anti-Russian_sentiment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: United States">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Russia%E2%80%93United_States_relations" title="Russia–United States relations">Russia–United States relations</a></div>
<p>According to a 2013 Poll, 59% of Americans had a negative view of Russia, 23% had a favorable opinion, and 18% were uncertain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WPO_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-WPO-83">[83]</a></sup> According to a survey by <a href="/wiki/Pew_Research_Center" title="Pew Research Center">Pew Research Center</a>, negative attitudes towards Russia in the United States rose from 43% to 72% from 2013 to 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pew-attitudes-2014_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-pew-attitudes-2014-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<p>Recent events such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anti-Magnitsky_bill" title="Anti-Magnitsky bill">Anti-Magnitsky bill</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boston_Marathon_bombings" title="Boston Marathon bombings">Boston Marathon bombings</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> and Russia's actions following the <a href="/wiki/Ukrainian_crisis" title="Ukrainian crisis">Ukrainian crisis</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pew-attitudes-2014_32-2"><a href="#cite_note-pew-attitudes-2014-32">[32]</a></sup> are deemed to have caused negative impression about Russia in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup></p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Hollywood">Hollywood</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anti-Russian_sentiment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Hollywood">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>In a 2014 news story, <i>Fox News</i> reported, "Russians may also be unimpressed with Hollywood’s apparent negative stereotyping of Russians in movies. <i>"<a href="/wiki/The_Avengers_(2012_film)" title="The Avengers (2012 film)">The Avengers</a>"</i> featured a ruthless former <a href="/wiki/KGB" title="KGB">KGB</a> agent, <i>"<a href="/wiki/Iron_Man_2" title="Iron Man 2">Iron Man 2</a>"</i> centers on a rogue Russian scientist with a vendetta, and action thriller <i>"<a href="/wiki/Jack_Ryan:_Shadow_Recruit" title="Jack Ryan: Shadow Recruit">Jack Ryan: Shadow Recruit</a>"</i> saw Kenneth Branagh play an archetypal Russian bad guy, just to name a few."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Business">Business</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anti-Russian_sentiment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Business">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In May and June 2006, Russian media cited discrimination against Russian companies as one possible reason why the contemplated merger between the Luxembourg-based steelmaker <i><a href="/wiki/Arcelor" title="Arcelor">Arcelor</a></i> and Russia's <i><a href="/wiki/Severstal" title="Severstal">Severstal</a></i> did not finalize. According to the Russian daily <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Izvestiya" title="Izvestiya">Izvestiya</a>, those opposing the merger <i>"exploited the 'Russian threat' myth during negotiations with shareholders and, apparently, found common ground with the Europeans"</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> while <a href="/wiki/Boris_Gryzlov" title="Boris Gryzlov">Boris Gryzlov</a>, speaker of the <a href="/wiki/State_Duma" title="State Duma">State Duma</a> observed that <i>"recent events show that someone does not want to allow us to enter their markets."</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> On 27 July 2006, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_York_Times" title="New York Times">New York Times</a> quoted the analysts as saying that many Western investors still think that anything to do with Russia is <i>"a little bit doubtful and dubious"</i> while others look at Russia in <i>"comic book terms, as mysterious and mafia-run."</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="View_of_Russia_in_Western_media">View of Russia in Western media</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anti-Russian_sentiment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: View of Russia in Western media">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Some Russian and Western commentators express concern about a far too negative coverage of Russia in Western media (some Russians even describe this as a "war of information").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> In April 2007, David Johnson, founder of the <a href="/wiki/Johnson%27s_Russia_List" title="Johnson's Russia List">Johnson's Russia List</a>, said in interview to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moscow_News" title="Moscow News">Moscow News</a>: "I am sympathetic to the view that these days Putin and Russia are perhaps getting too dark a portrayal in most Western media. Or at least that critical views need to be supplemented with other kinds of information and analysis. An openness to different views is still warranted."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1995, years before <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Putin" title="Vladimir Putin">Vladimir Putin</a> was elected to his first term, the Pew Research Center for the People and the Press reported: "coverage of Russia and its president, <a href="/wiki/Boris_Yeltsin" title="Boris Yeltsin">Boris Yeltsin</a>, was decidedly negative, even though national polls continue to find the public feeling positive toward Russia and largely uncritical of Yeltsin."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup></p>
<p>In February 2007, the Russian creativity agency <a class="extiw" href="https://ru.wikipedia.org/wiki/E-generator" title="ru:E-generator">E-generator</a> put together a "rating of Russophobia" of Western media, using for the research articles concerning a single theme—Russia's chairmanship of G8, translated into Russian by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/InoSmi.Ru" title="InoSmi.Ru">InoSmi.Ru</a>. The score was composed for each edition, negative values granted for negative assessments of Russia, and positive values representing positive ones. The top in the rating were <i><a href="/wiki/Newsday" title="Newsday">Newsday</a></i> (−43, U.S.), <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Financial_Times" title="The Financial Times">The Financial Times</a></i> (−34, Great Britain), <i><a href="/wiki/The_Wall_Street_Journal" title="The Wall Street Journal">The Wall Street Journal</a></i> (−34, U.S.), <i><a href="/wiki/Le_Monde" title="Le Monde">Le Monde</a></i> (−30, France), while editions on the opposite side of the rating were <i><a href="/wiki/Toronto_Star" title="Toronto Star">Toronto Star</a></i> (+27, Canada) and <i>"The Conservative Voice"</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> (+26, U.S.).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a>-based <a href="/wiki/International_relations" title="International relations">international relations</a> scholar <a href="/wiki/Andrei_Tsygankov" title="Andrei Tsygankov">Andrei Tsygankov</a> has remarked that anti-Russian political rhetoric coming from <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington</a> circles has received wide echo in American mainstream media, asserting that "Russophobia's revival is indicative of the fear shared by some U.S. and European politicians that their grand plans to control the world's most precious resources and geostrategic sites may not succeed if Russia's economic and political recovery continues."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tsygankov.C2.A0.E2.80.94_May_2008_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tsygankov.C2.A0.E2.80.94_May_2008-123">[123]</a></sup></p>
<p>In practice, anti-Russian political rhetoric usually puts emphasis on highlighting policies and practices of the Russian government that are criticised internally - <a href="/wiki/Corruption_in_Russia" title="Corruption in Russia">corruption</a>, abuse of law, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Freedom_of_the_press_in_Russia" title="Freedom of the press in Russia">censorship</a>, violence and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2014%E2%80%9315_Russian_military_intervention_in_Ukraine" title="2014–15 Russian military intervention in Ukraine">intervention in Ukraine</a>. Western criticism in this aspect goes in line with Russian independent anti-government media such as (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/TV_Rain" title="TV Rain">TV Rain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Novaya_Gazeta" title="Novaya Gazeta">Novaya Gazeta</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ekho_Moskvy" title="Ekho Moskvy">Ekho Moskvy</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Moscow_Times" title="The Moscow Times">The Moscow Times</a>) and opposition human rights activists (<a href="/wiki/Memorial_(society)" title="Memorial (society)">Memorial</a>). In defence of this rhetoric, some sources critical of the Russian government claim that it is Russian state-owned media and administration who attempt to discredit the "neutral" criticism by generalizing it into indiscriminate accusations of the whole Russian population - or russophobia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jdpz_11-2"><a href="#cite_note-jdpz-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup></p>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Media_portrayal_of_the_Ukrainian_crisis" title="Media portrayal of the Ukrainian crisis">Media portrayal of the Ukrainian crisis</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Freedom_of_the_press_in_Russia" title="Freedom of the press in Russia">Freedom of the press in Russia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Trolls_from_Olgino" title="Trolls from Olgino">Trolls from Olgino</a></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Russian_nationalist_ideology">Russian nationalist ideology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anti-Russian_sentiment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Russian nationalist ideology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The issue of anti-Russian sentiment has become an indispensable part of contemporary <a href="/wiki/Russian_nationalism" title="Russian nationalism">Russian nationalist</a> ideology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jdpz_11-3"><a href="#cite_note-jdpz-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Horvath_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-Horvath-126">[126]</a></sup> Sociologist <a href="/wiki/Anatoly_Khazanov" title="Anatoly Khazanov">Anatoly Khazanov</a> states that there's a national-patriotic movement which believes that there's a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clash_of_civilizations" title="Clash of civilizations">clash of civilizations</a>, a global struggle between the <a href="/wiki/Eurasianism" title="Eurasianism">idealist, collectivist, morally and spiritually superior Eurasia led by Russia</a>, and the corrupt, materialistic, decadent, morally and spiritually polluted West led by the United States.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>This national-patriotic movement charges that for centuries the West has plundered Russia of her natural resources, trying to bring Russia to her knees and undermining Russia's messianic and self-sacrificing mission to enlighten and save mankind, with the collapse of the Soviet Union as evidence.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> In their view, the United States want to break up Russia and turn it into a source of raw materials. The West being accused of Russophobia is a major part of their belief.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anti-Russian_sentiment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Footnotes">Footnotes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anti-Russian_sentiment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Footnotes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-1">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a class="external text" href="http://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/russophobia" rel="nofollow">"Russophobia"</a>. <i>Merriam Webster</i>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAnti-Russian+sentiment&amp;rft.atitle=Russophobia&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.merriam-webster.com%2Fdictionary%2Frussophobia&amp;rft.jtitle=Merriam+Webster&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-prevalence-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-prevalence_2-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation news"><a class="external text" href="https://wayback.archive.org/web/20080221194733/http://uk.reuters.com/article/topNews/idUKL0824162420070708" rel="nofollow">"Envoy complains Britons mistreat Russians"</a>.
